Chapter 1 Introductory Material 1
1.1 Scope 2 This Technical Specification has been produced by the O-RAN.org. 3 The contents of the present document are subject to continuing work within O-RAN WG4 and may change following 4 formal O-RAN approval. Should the O-RAN.org modify the contents of the present document, it will be re-released by 5 O-RAN Alliance with an identifying change of release date and an increase in version number as follows: 6 Release x.y.z 7 where: 8 x the first digit is incremented for all changes of substance, i.e. technical enhancements, corrections, updates, 9 etc. (the initial approved document will have x=01). 10 y the second digit is incremented when editorial only changes have been incorporated in the document. 11 z the third digit included only in working versions of the document indicating incremental changes during the 12 editing process. 13 The present document specifies the management plane protocols used over the fronthaul interface linking the O-RU (O-14 RAN Radio Unit) with other management plane entities, that may include the O-DU (O-RAN Distributed Unit) as well 15 as other Network Management Systems (NMS).  16 1.2 References 17 The following documents contain provisions which, through reference in this text, constitute provisions of the present 18 document. 19 - References are either specific (identified by date of publication, edition number, version number, etc.) or 20 non-specific. 21 - For a specific reference, subsequent revisions do not apply. 22 - For a non-specific reference, the latest version applies. In the case of a reference to a 3GPP document (including 23 a GSM document), a non-specific reference implicitly refers to the latest version of that document in Release 15. 24 [1] 3GPP TR 21.905: “Vocabulary for 3GPP Specifications” 25 [2] ORAN-WG4.CUS.0-v01 “Control, User and Synchronization Plane Specification”, O-RAN  26 Alliance, Working Group 4  27 [3] RFC 6241, “Network Configuration Protocol (NETCONF)”, IETF, June 2011 28 [4] RFC 7950, “The YANG 1.1 Data Modeling Language”, IETF, August 2016 29 [5] RFC 6242, “Using the NETCONF Protocol over Secure Shell (SSH)”, IETF, June 2011 30 [6] RFC 4252, “The Secure Shell (SSH) Authentication Protocol”, IETF, January 2006 31 [7] RFC 4253, “The Secure Shell (SSH) Transport Layer Protocol”, IETF, January 2006 32 [8] RFC 2132, “DHCP Options and BOOTP Vendor Extensions”, IETF, March 1997 33 [9] RFC 3925, “Vendor-Identifying Vendor Options for Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol version 34 4 (DHCPv4)”, IETF, October 2004 35 [10] RFC 2131, “Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol”, IETF, March 1997 36 [11] RFC 4862, “IPv6 Stateless Address Autoconfiguration”, IETF, September 2007 37 [12] RFC 3315, “Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol for IPv6 (DHCPv6)”, IETF, July 2003 38 [13] RFC 3736, “Stateless Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol (DHCP) Service for IPv6”, IETF, April 39 2004 40 [14] draft-ietf-netconf-zerotouch, “Zero Touch Provisioning for NETCONF or RESTCONF based 41 Management”, IETF, work in progress 42

ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 [15] RFC 8071, “NETCONF Call Home and RESTCONF Call Home”, IETF, February 2017 1 [16] SFF-8472v11, “Diagnostic Monitoring Interface for Optical Transceivers”, SFF Committee, 2 September 2010 3 [17] IEEE 802.1ag, “IEEE Standard for Local and Metropolitan Area Networks Virtual Bridged Local 4 Area Networks Amendment 5: Connectivity Fault Management”, IEEE, 2007 5 [18] RFC 862, “Echo Protocol”, IETF, May 1983 6 [19] MEF.38, “Service OAM Fault management YANG Modules”, Metro Ethernet Forum, April 2012 7 [20] RFC 7895, “YANG Model Library”, IETF, June 2016  8 [21] RFC 5277, “NETCONF Event Notifications”, IETF, July 2008 9 [22] G.8275.1, “Precision time protocol telecom profile for phase/time synchronization with full timing 10 support from the network”, ITU, June 2016 11 [23] G.810, “Definitions and terminology for synchronization networks”, ITU, August 1996 12 [24] 1588v2-2008, “IEEE Standard for a Precision Clock Synchronization Protocol for Networked 13 Measurement and Control Systems”, IEEE, 2008 14 [25] Y.1731, “Operation, administration and maintenance (OAM) functions and mechanisms for 15 Ethernet based networks”, ITU, August 2015 16 [26] AISG 2.0, “Control interface for antenna line devices”, Antenna Interface Standards Group, June 17 2006 18 [27] 3GPP 25.462, “UTRAN Iuant interface: Signalling transport”, 3GPP 19 [28] 3GPP 25.466, “UTRAN Iuant interface: Application part”, 3GPP 20 [29] 3GPP 25.463, “UTRAN Iuant interface: Remote Electrical Tilting (RET) antennas Application Part 21 (RETAP) signalling”, 3GPP 22 [30] ITU X.733, “Information Technology – Open Systems Interconnection - System Management: 23 Alarm Reporting Function”, 1992 24 [31] RFC 6187, “X.509v3 Certificates for Secure Shell Authentication”, IETF, March 2011 25 [32] 3GPP TS 36.213, “Evolved Universal Terrestrial Radio Access (E-UTRA); Physical layer 26 procedures”, 3GPP, V13.6.0 (2017-06) 27 [33] RFC 4361, Node-specific Client Identifiers for Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol Version Four 28 (DHCPv4), IETF, February 2006 29 [34] SFF-8636v2.9.3, “Specification for Management Interface for Cabled Environment”, SFF 30 Committee, April 2019 31  32 1.3 Definitions and Abbreviations 33 1.3.1 Definitions 34 For the purposes of the present document, the terms and definitions given in 3GPP TR 21.905 [1] and the following apply. 35 A term defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same term, if any, in 3GPP 36 TR 21.905 [1]. 37 Antenna Line: connection between O-RU and antenna 38 C-Plane: Control Plane: refers specifically to real-time control between O-DU and O-RU, and should not be confused 39 with the UE’s control plane 40 c_eAxC: component eAxC: a portion of an eAxC flow assigned to a specific O-DU processing element.  41 DL: DownLink: data flow towards the radiating antenna (generally on the LLS interface) 42 eAxC: extended Antenna-Carrier: a data flow for a single antenna (or spatial stream) for a single carrier in a single sector. 43

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        9
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 LLS: Lower Layer Split: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer (intra-PHY based) 1 functional split. 2 LLS-U: Lower Layer Split User-plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer functional 3 split. 4 LLS-C: Lower Layer Split Control-plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer functional 5 split. 6 LLS-S: Lower Layer Split Synchronization-plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer 7 functional split. 8 High-PHY: those portions of the PHY processing on the O-DU side of the fronthaul interface, including FEC 9 encode/decode, scrambling, and modulation/demodulation. 10 Low-PHY: those portions of the PHY processing on the O-RU side of the fronthaul interface, including FFT/iFFT, digital 11 beamforming, and PRACH extraction and filtering. 12 M-Plane: Management Plane: refers to non-real-time management operations between the O-DU and the O-RU 13 NMS: A Network Management System dedicated to O-RU operations 14 Port: End of a transport link – in most cases this is an optical port 15 Port Number: A number which identifies a port (see Port). In case of SFP/SFP+ port, port number value is 0 to N-1 16 where N is number of ports in the device. Numbers 0 to N-1 are assigned to ports in order following order of labels on 17 the device (labels for ports are not necessarily numbers starting from zero) 18 O-DU: O-RAN Distributed Unit: a logical node hosting PDCP/RLC/MAC/High-PHY layers based on a lower layer 19 functional split. 20 O-RU: O-RAN Radio Unit: a logical node hosting Low-PHY layer and RF processing based on a lower layer functional 21 split.  This is similar to 3GPP’s “TRP” or “RRH” but more specific in including the Low-PHY layer (FFT/iFFT, PRACH 22 extraction).   23 O-RU Controller: A network function that is permitted to control the configuration of an O-RU. Examples of O-RU 24 controllers include, an O-DU, a classical NMS, or other network automation platforms.  25 S-Plane: Synchronization Plane: refers to traffic between the O-RU or O-DU to a synchronization controller which is 26 generally an IEEE-1588 Grand Master (however, Grand Master functionality may be embedded in the O-DU). 27 Spatial stream: the data flow on the DL associated with precoded data (may be same as layers or different if there is 28 expansion in the precoding), and on UL associated with the number of outputs from the digital beamforming (sometimes 29 called “beams”).  30 SSM: Synchronization Status Message: part of ITU G.781 and G.8264 standards. 31 TRX: Refers to the specific processing chain in an O-RU associated with D/A or A/D converters.  Due to digital 32 beamforming the number of TRXs may exceed the number of spatial streams, and due to analog beamforming the number 33 of TRXs may be lower than the number of antenna elements. 34 U-Plane: User Plane: refers to IQ sample data transferred between O-DU and O-RU 35 UL: UpLink: data flow away from the radiating antenna (generally on the LLS interface) 36 Virtual Connection: a connection between O-RU and O-RU controller. This connection is established by means of 37 autodetection procedure and is supervised by supervision procedure. 38 1.3.2 Abbreviations 39 For the purposes of the present document, the abbreviations given in 3GPP TR 21.905 [1] and the following apply. An 40 abbreviation defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same abbreviation, if any, in 41 3GPP TR 21.905 [1]. 42 ALD Antenna Line Device 43 AVP Average Power 44 BCN BTS Clock Number 45 CRC Cyclic Redundancy Check 46

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        10
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 CUS Control/User/Synchronization 1 DHCP Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol 2 DMTC DRS Measurement Timing Configuration 3 DRS Discovery Reference Signal 4 DSCP Differentiated Services Code Point  5 HDLC High-Level Data Link Control 6 lls-M Lower Layer Split Management plane 7 LAA Licensed Assisted Access 8 LBM Loop-Back Message 9 LBR Loop Back Reply 10 LBT Listen Before Talk  11 ME  Maintenance Entity 12 MEP Maintenance association End Point 13 NAT Network Address Translation 14 NETCONF Network Configuration Protocol 15 O-DU O-RAN Distributed Unit (see definitions section) 16 O-RU O-RAN Radio Unit 17 OMA Optical Modulation Amplitude 18 PDV Packet Delay Variation 19 QoS Quality of Service 20 RET Remote Electrical Tilt 21 RPC Remote Procedure Call 22 SFP Small Form-factor Pluggable 23 sFTP Secure File Transfer Protocol or SSH File Transfer Protocol 24 SLAAC Stateless Address Auto Configuration 25 SSH Secure Shell 26 VLAN Virtual LAN 27 YANG Yet Another Next Generation 28  29 1.4 Conventions 30 This management plane specification includes cross references to a set of associated YANG models. Text may reference 31 particular YANG leafs, notifications and remote procedure calls (RPCs). In order to assist in readability, all cross 32 references to YANG defined elements will keep the identical case format as defined in the corresponding YANG model, 33 with the font-weight set to bold. This convention applies only to text and not to YANG elements embedded into figures.  34 If there is any conflict between the YANG models and the accompanying text description in this specification, the 35 definition of the YANG models shall take precedence. 36
1.5 Topics for Future Specification Versions 37 Extensions to this version of the O-RAN WG4 Management Plane specification together with corrected errors will be 38 included in the future versions of this document.  39  40

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        11
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 The following topics are to be considered for future versions of the specification: 1 1. Beam Id field interpretation for various types of beamforming 2 2. Redundancy and failover scenario 3  4 The revision statement in the YANG models will be used to describe future revisions to the models that are backwards 5 compatible. Backwards incompatible changes will be addressed by incrementing the number used as part of the model 6 name and namespace, effectively creating a new YANG model. The format of the namespace used in all O-RAN YANG 7 models is “urn:o-ran:”<model-name>“:”<model-number>, where the initial <model-number> used in a newly defined 8 YANG model is “1.0”. Where this document makes reference to models, irrespective of their backward compatibity, a 9 generic <model-number> of “x.y” is used to enable reference to all versions of the namespace for a particular <model-10 name>. 11  12 The revision statement in all YANG models includes a reference statement used to cross-reference to the first version of 13 this document where the corresponding description was introduced. For example, the reference in all revision statements 14 for the initial O-RAN models include cross-reference to “ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v01.00”.  15  16 The revision statement of the YANG models also includes a description which is used to track the versioning of the 17 YANG model. All revision statement descriptions will begin with “version ”<a>“.”<b>“.”<c>, where <a>, <b> and <c> 18 are used to reflect the version of the YANG model, where 19  20 <a> corresponds to the first digit of the O-RAN WG4 management plane specification version where the 21 corresponding description was first introduced, corresponding to <x> in sub-section 1.1 22 <b>  is incremented when errors in the YANG model have been corrected 23 <c> is incremented only in working versions of the the YANG model indicating incremental changes during the 24 editing process 25  26  27
Chapter 2 High Level Description 28
2.1 Top level functional description, terminology, including hybrid, 29 hierarchical 30 2.1.1 Architecture for O-RAN WG4 Fronthaul functional split 31 This O-RAN FH specification addresses the lower layer functional split as depicted in Figure 1. Refer to the O-RAN CUS 32 plane specification [2] for more details on the split architecture. The Lower-Layer Split M-plane (LLS-M) facilitates the 33 initialization, configuration and management of the O-RU to support the stated functional split. 34

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        12
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00
 1 Figure 1 – O-RAN WG4 FH functional split 2 2.1.2 M-Plane architecture model 3 A NETCONF/YANG based M-Plane is used for supporting the management features including “start up” installation, 4 software management, configuration management, performance management, fault management and file management 5 towards the O-RU. The M-Plane supports two architectural models: 6 1. Hierarchical model.  As shown on the left side Figure 1, the O-RU is managed entirely by one or more O-DU(s) 7 using a NETCONF based M-Plane interface. When the O-RU is managed by multiple O-DUs, it is typically for 8 enabling O-DU and/or transport connectivity redundancy capabilities. Refer to Chapter 3 for more details. 9 2. Hybrid model. As shown on the right side of Figure 1, the hybrid architecture enables one or more direct logical 10 interface(s) between management system(s) and O-RU in addition to a logical interface between O-DU and the O-11 RU. It should be noted that the NETCONF clients connecting to the O-RU may be of different classes (e.g. O-DU and 12 NMS). For example, functions like O-RU software management, performance management, configuration 13 management and fault management can be managed directly by the management system(s).  14 In the hybrid model, the O-RU has end to end IP layer connectivity with the NMS. From a physical network point of 15 view, this connectivity could be via the O-DU, where the O-DU is acting as an IP/Ethernet packet forwarder, forwards 16 the packets between O-RU and the NMS. Direct logical communication between an O-RU and NMS can be enabled via 17 O-RUs being assigned routable IPs or local private IPs resolved by a NAT function in the network (or implemented at 18 the O-DU). Refer to chapter 3 for details how O-RU acquires the IP address of O-DU and NMS for the M-plane 19 communication. 20 As described in Chapter 3, there is no explicit signaling to indicate that an O-RU is operating in a hierarchical or hybrid 21 configuration. All NETCONF servers supporting this M-Plane specification shall support multiple NETCONF sessions, 22 and hence all complaint O-RUs shall be able to support both hierarchical and hybrid deployment. 23

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        13
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00
 1 Figure 2 - M-Plane Architecture 2 NETCONF/YANG is used as the network element management protocol [3] and data modeling language [4]. Use of such 3 a standardized framework and common modeling language simplifies integration between O-DU and O-RU as well as 4 operator network integration (in terms of running service) in case of elements sharing a common set of capabilities. The 5 framework supports integration of products with differing capabilities enabled by well-defined published data models. 6 NETCONF also natively supports a hybrid architecture which enables multiple clients to subscribe and receive 7 information originating at the NETCONF server in the O-RU. 8 2.1.3 Transport Network 9 Based on the transport topology, various modes of network connectivity are possible between O-RU and O-DU and NMS.  10 The basic requirement for M-Plane is to have end to end IP connectivity between the O-RU and the elements managing 11 it (O-DU, NMS, or so called “O-RU Controllers”). The connectivity between the O-DU and NMS and its management 12 plane are not in scope of this specification. IPv4 shall be supported as a mandatory transport protocol for M-Plane and 13 IPv6 support is optional. The M-Plane shall be NAT agnostic for the O-RU to O-DU/NMS connectivity, in order to 14 support a variety of possible network and IP addressing deployments.  15 2.1.4 M-Plane functional description 16 The M-Plane provides the following major functionalities to the O-RU. These features are implemented using the 17 NETCONF provided functions. 18  “Start up” installation  19 During startup, the O-RU acquires its network layer parameters either via static (pre-configured in the O-RU) or 20 dynamically via DHCP or DHCPv6. During this process it also acquires the IP address of the O-RU controller(s), and 21 establishes the NETCONF connectivity using the “call home” feature. The capability exchange is performed between the 22 client and server as part of the initial NETCONF Hello exchanges. Details of these steps are provided in chapter 3. 23

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        14
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 SW management  1 The M-Plane is responsible for software download, installation, validation and activation of new SW when requested by 2 O-RU Controller. The software download is triggered by NETCONF RPC procedures, and the actual software package 3 download is done using sFTP. In this version of the M-Plane specification, sFTP is the only defined file transfer protocol 4 for software and file management. 5 Configuration management 6 Configuration management covers various scenarios like Retrieve Resource State, Modify Resource State, Modify 7 Parameters and Retrieve Parameters. NETCONF get-config and edit-config RPCs shall be used for configuration 8 parameter retrieval and updates at the O-RU  9 Performance management 10 Performance management describes the measurements and counters used to collect data related to O-RU operations. The 11 purpose of Performance Management is optimizing the operation of the O-RU.  12 The measurement results are reported by two options:  13 1. YANG Notification: This option uses the stats definition of YANG model per measurement group. In this case, 14 get rpc and/or notification will be used (see Chapter 7 for more details).  15 2. File Upload: This option uses the file upload procedure defined in File management. The measurement results 16 are saved to a data file periodically.  17 Fault Management 18 Fault management is responsible for sending alarm notifications to the NETCONF Client. Fault Management allows 19 alarm notifications to be disabled or enabled as well as alarm subscription. 20 File Management 21 File management allows the O-RU Controller to trigger an O-RU to perform upload of files stored on O-RU to O-RU 22 Controller. The O-RU may provide different kinds of files and retrieved files can be used for various purposes. 23 Simultaneous multiple file upload operations can be supported under the same sFTP connection between O-RU to O-24 DU/NMS. 25
2.2 Interfaces 26 The M-Plane interface is defined between the O-DU/NMS and the O-RU. The protocol stack of the M-Plane interface is 27 shown in Figure 3. The transport network layer is built on IP transport and TCP/SSH is used to carry the M-Plane message 28 between the DU/NMS and the O-RU. 29

2.3 YANG Module Introduction 1 The data models representing the M-Plane are organized as a set of reusable YANG modules. It is also the intent to reuse 2 the publicly available and generic YANG models as much as possible instead of developing customized O-RAN specific 3 modules. Refer to the various chapters, Annex D and the repository of YANG models for more details on each of these 4 modules.  5
2.4 Security 6 The M-Plane provides end to end security as a mandatory feature. The security is provided using the SSHv2 layer in 7 accordance with RFC 6242 [5]. RFC 6242 provides the procedure for interoperability with NETCONF implementations. 8 If there are multiple NETCONF sessions established with a single O-RU, each session should be established over a 9 separate SSH tunnel. 10 Plane Integrity (protection from modifications) Confidentiality (encryption protection) Authentication (validity of the originator) Remarks
M-Plane Yes Yes Yes Using the SSHv2 layer used for NETCONF transport Table 1- M-Plane Security 11 SSHv2 performs server host authentication, key exchange, encryption, and integrity protection. It also derives a unique 12 session ID that may be used by higher-level protocols. The end point authentication should be done as per RFC 4252 [6]. 13 Chapter 3 describes the authentication approach based on username and password as well as based on X.509 certificates. 14 The SSHv2 transport level security (encryption algorithms, data integrity algorithms) shall be based on RFC4253 [7]. As 15 per this RFC, 3des-cbc shall be the mandatory cyphering protocol, and rest of the ones listed as optional. For data integrity, 16 hmac-sha1 shall be the mandatory algorithm, and rest of the listed algorithms shall be optional. Public key-based host 17 authentication shall be used for authenticating the server (RFC 4253) by the clients, and username/password based client 18 authentication shall be done by the server as part of the SSH session establishment.  19 As an additional option, both client and server may implement authentication based on X.509 certificates. With this 20 option, RSA 2048 bit shall be supported for the Public Key algorithm, aes-cbc shall be supported for the cyphering 21 algorithm and hmac-sha256 shall be supported for integrity algorithm. 22 Note: FIPS-186-3 introduces longer keys but as only FIPS/186-2 is referred in RFC4253 and SHA-1 is used, then key 23 length defined in FIPS-186-2 shall be supported. 24

3.1 Management Plane Transport aspects 1 This sub-section provides the M-plane transport establishment scenario between O-RU and O-RU controller(s), such as 2 O-DU and/or NMS. The transport layer address of M-plane is only the target in this section. Transport aspects of the C 3 plane and U plane are covered in Chapter 4. 4 Pre-condition:  5 - Physical interface is connected. 6 - The NETCONF server and NETCONF Client(s) have an identical NETCONF call home port configured, to ensure 7 the NETCONF client listens on the same port used by the NETCONF Server. 8 Post-condition:   9 - Transport Layer address(es) for M-plane are known to O-RU and controllers.  10 - O-RU is aware of the physical port(s) for M-plane, e.g., if there are multiple ports in the O-RU. 11 - O-RU is aware of the VLAN(s) to be used for M-Plane, e.g., if VLANs are used in the transport network. 12 - Then O-RU is ready to establish TCP connection for NETCONF call home. 13 For the transport establishment, there are the following alternatives: 14 a) Manual transport layer address configuration in O-RU. This configuration contains the addresses for O-RU and 15 NETCONF client(s). The method to manually configure the O-RU is out of scope in this specification. Assuming manual 16 configuration is successful, the NETCONF server shall be able to recover this configured information and use the o-ran-17 mplane-int.yang model to communicate this operational-state to a NETCONF client. 18 b) DHCP server provides O-RU’s transport layer address information together with the identity of the NETCONF client. 19 This identity encodes either the transport layer address or FQDN of the NETCONF client. If an FQDN is signaled, the 20 O-RU shall use the DNS server address provided by the DHCP server to recover the IP address corresponding to FQDN 21 of the NETCONF client. 22 c) If IPv6 is supported, Stateless Address Auto-Configuration (SLAAC) is used to configure the O-RU’s transport 23 address with the DHCP server providing the identity of the NETCONF client. This identity encodes either the transport 24 layer address or FQDN of the NETCONF client. If an FQDN is signaled, the O-RU shall use the DNS server address 25 provided by the DHCP server to recover the IP address corresponding to FQDN of the NETCONF client.  26 Note, a NETCONF client can determine whether an O-RU supports IPv6 by using the get rpc to recover the list of 27 interfaces supported by the O-RU and using the presence of the augmented ipv6 container in the o-ran-interfaces module 28 to indicate IPv6 is supported. 29 The O-RU uses the o-ran-dhcp.yang model to be able to expose information signaled by the DHCP server. 30

If the O-RU supports IPv6, then it shall identify itself using the DHCPv6 Vendor Class Option. 9 DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option: 10 • Option: 60 11 • Vendor Class Identifier Option 60: string  12 The format of the vendor class string shall be configured to one of the following three options:  13 1. ““o-ran-ru2/<vendor>, e.g., “o-ran-ru2/vendorA 14 2. “o-ran-ru2/<vendor>/<product-code>”, e.g., “o-ran-ru2/vendorA/ORUAA100 15 3. “o-ran-ru2/<vendor>/<product-code>/<serial-number>”,e.g. , “o-ran-16 ru2/vendorA/ORUAA100/FR1918010111 17 DHCPv4 Vendor-Identifying Vendor Class Option: 18 • Option: 124 19 • Enterprise number: O-RAN-alliance 53148 20 • Vendor-Class-Data: the format of the string shall follow the rules defined for the DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option 21 DHCPv6 Vendor Class Option: 22 • Option: 16 23 • Enterprise number: O-RAN-alliance 53148 24 • Vendor-Class-Data: the format of the string shall follow the rules defined for the DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option 25 The DHCP Server may use this information when allocating IP addresses or when configuring management plane O-RU 26 Controller(s) information in the O-RU. 27 3.1.2 Management Plane VLAN Discovery Aspects 28 The O-RU will be connected to one or more Ethernet ports. The transport systems may be realized such that these Ethernet 29 ports may be configured either as an access port, where untagged Ethernet frames are used, or as a trunk port, where 30 multiple VLANs are configured. At start up, the O-RU will typically not be able to immediately determine whether its 31 ports are attached to remote transport equipment configured for access or trunk mode operation.   32 Once an O-RU completes its boot-up sequence and Ethernet connectivity is detected on at least one of its Ethernet 33 interfaces, the O-RU starts management plane connection establishment. 34 The O-RU needs to determine whether it is connected to an access port or a trunk port. In particular, when connected to 35 a trunk port, the O-RU needs to additionally determine the VLAN identity/ies used to support the management plane 36 communication(s). The VLAN(s) used to support management plane communications can be identified by the DHCP 37 server replying to the DHCP DISCOVER message, as described in section 3.1.4. 38

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        21
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 Note, an O-RU which supports IPv6 may infer that a VLAN is not used to support management plane communications if 1 it receives an IPv6 Router Advertisement without either the “managed address configuration” or “other configuration” 2 bits set. 3 An O-RU may have been previously configured with management plane VLAN information, for example storing the last 4 VLAN(s) used for management plane connectivity, and/or being previously configured with a range of management plane 5 VLANs by a NETCONF client that has been stored in reset-persistent memory. The O-RU may use this information to 6 optimize its discovery of the VLAN ID(s) used for management plane connectivity. 7 If the O-RU does not have previously configured management plane VLAN information, the O-RU shall attempt to 8 discover DHCP servers on all of its Ethernet ports using untagged Ethernet frames.  9 If the O-RU does not receive a DHCP OFFER from a DHCP server using untagged frames, or previously configured 10 VLANs, the O-RU should attempt to contact a DHCP server using individual VLANs on all of its Ethernet ports. 11 3.1.3 O-RU Management Plane IP Address Assignment 12 Automatic IP address assignment for the O-RU management plane can be achieved using different techniques: 13 1. IPv4 configuration using DHCPv4, RFC2131 [10] enables DHCP servers to configure IPv4 network address(es) on 14 the O-RU. An O-RU shall support the behavior specified in RFC 4361 [33], using stable DHCPv4 node identifiers in their 15 dhcp-client-identifier option. 16 Note: a network realized with multiple DHCP servers should ensure that their configurations are co-ordinated to ensure 17 a common default gateway is provisioned in an O-RU which receives multiple DHCPv4 responses, e.g., when received 18 over different interfaces. 19 Note: an O-RU may indicate that it supports configuration of routing information using RFC 3442, enabling static routes 20 to be used by the O-RU when determining how to route uplink packets, e.g., when the O-RU supports multiple interfaces. 21 For O-RUs that support IPv6, both stateful and stateless address assignment procedures are supported: 22 2. IPv6 Stateless Address Auto-Configuration (SLAAC), RFC4862 [11] enables the O-RU to generate link-local and 23 global addresses. 24 Note: a network realized with multiple IPv6-enabled routers that support dynamic address assignment is expected to use 25 RFC 4191 to configure the preference of the default route prefixes learnt by the O-RU using SLAAC. 26 3. IPv6 State-full address configuration uses DHCPv6, RFC3315 [12] and enables DHCP servers to configure IPv6 27 network address(es) on the O-RU. DHCPv6 is transported using UDP, using the link-local address on the O-RU and a 28 link-scoped multicast address on the DHCP server.  29 Note: the above does not restrict the realization of the DHCP server, which may be integrated with the O-DU, may be 30 provided by the transport system, or may be accessed via a relay. 31 The DHCP server should operate using static bindings, i.e., ensuring an O-RU identified by a particular client hardware 32 address will be re-allocated the same management plane IP address, e.g., after performing an O-RU reset procedure. 33 3.1.4 O-RU Controller Discovery 34 This section provides how to automatically discover the O-RU Controller address(es). 35 O-RUs that have obtained their IPv6 addresses by stateless address auto-configuration, shall use stateless DHCPv6, 36 RFC3736 [13], to obtain NETCONF management plane configuration information.  37 Other O-RUs operating using stateful IPv4 or IPv6 address allocations shall obtain NETCONF management plane 38 configuration information during IP address allocation. 39 The O-RU as NETCONF Server shall be able to recover NETCONF Client information using the following DHCP 40 Options, draft-ietf-netconf-zerotouch [14]:  41 • DHCPv4 OPTION V4_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT [143] 42 • DHCPv6 OPTION_V6_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT [136] 43

ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 These options are defined in [14] and are used to deliver bootstrap-server-list information to the O-RU. The O-RU shall 1 use these options to recover the NETCONF client information using the above IANA defined DHCP options. The O-RU 2 is not required to implement the remainder of the zerotouch capabilities defined in [14].  3 The above DHCP option provided information is encoded as a list of one or more server URIs, of the format “https://<ip-4 address-or-hostname>[:<port>]” signaled to the O-RU. The DHCP server shall ensure that all NETCONF client 5 information is encoded with these options. 6 Other O-RUs which have had their IP address(es) manually configured, shall also have their O-RU Controller(s) manually 7 configured. 8 For IPv4, the O-RU may request the OPTION_V4_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT by including its option code in the 9 Parameter Request List (55) in DHCP discover/request messages. 10 For IPv6, the O-RU may request the OPTION_V6_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT option by including the requested option 11 codes in the Option Request Option. 12 Note, these operations are optional because the DHCP server will already be aware that it is communicating with an O-13 RU based on the recovered vendor class option. 14 To enable O-RUs to operate in legacy environments where the DHCP server has not been enhanced with IANA defined 15 DHCP options for zero touch NETCONF capability, an O-RAN defined vendor specific option can be used to signal all 16 NETCONF client information to the O-RU using option 43 for DHCPv4 and option 17 for DHCPv6. Multiple instances 17 of NETCONF client information may be signaled, encoded as a sequence of type/length/value fields. 18 The definition of the types used within the DHCPv4 option 43/DHCPv6 Option 17 depends on the vendor-class option 19 reported by the O-RU in its DHCP messages. 20 When a legacy O-RU reports its vendor-class using the “o-ran-ru” prefix, the following types are defined: 21 Type: 0x01 – O-RU Controller IP Address 22 Type: 0x02 – O-RU Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name 23 When the O-RU reports its vendor-class using the “o-ran-ru2” prefix, the following types are defined: 24 Type: 0x81 – O-RU Controller IP Address 25 Type 0x82 – O-RU Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name 26 In all cases, the Type is followed by the length, which is the hexadecimal encoding of length of value field in octets, and 27 the Value. 28 When Type corresponds to an O-RU Controller IP Address, the value encodes IPv4 address(es) in hexadecimal format. 29 For example, a single server with IPv4 address 198.185.159.144 will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as 30 Type 0x81 (or x01 for legacy) 31 Length: 0x04  32 Value: C6 B9 9F 90 33 When Type corresponds to an O-RU Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name, this encodes the string representation of 34 domain name, using ACSII encoding (i.e., following for encoding used for the domain name in the Host Name DHCP 35 Option 12). For example, a server with FQDN “controller.operator.com” will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as 36 Type 0x82 (or x02 for legacy)  37 Length: 0x17  38 Value: 63 6F 6E 74 72 6F 6C 6C 65 72 2E 6F 70 65 72 61 74 6F 72 2E 63 6F 6D 39 The format of the DHCPv6 option 17 follows the format of the DHCPv4 encoding, with the additional inclusion of an 40 Enterprise Number prior to the TLV option data. 3.1.5 Multi-Vendor Plug-and-Play 1 As described in sub-section 3.3.2, an O-RU may optionally support certificate enrollment using CMPv2. 3GPP 32.509 2 specifies how the O-RU can discover the IP address or FQDN of one or more Certification Authority (CA/RA) servers 3 using DHCP Option 43. 4 In addition to the DHCPv4 encoding using Option 43, an O-RU supporting IPv6 and certificate enrollment using 5 CMPv2 shall additionally support the signaling of vendor specific options using DHCPv6 option 17. The format of the 6 DHCPv6 option 17 follows the format of the DHCPv4 encoding, with the additional inclusion of an Enterprise Number 7 prior to the TLV option data. The IANA allocated private enterprise number to be used with DHCPv6 option 17 is 8 53148 (as allocated by IANA to O-RAN Alliance). 9 An O-RU shall report any discovered multi-vendor plug-and-play servers using the o-ran-dhcp YANG model. 10
3.2 NETCONF Call Home to O-RU Controller(s) 11 The O-RU aims to have NETCONF sessions with all of the known O-RU Controller(s), either discovered using the DHCP 12 options defined in Section 3.1.4, provisioned by an existing NETCONF client, or statically configured. An O-RU 13 controller may attempt to autonomously initiate a NETCONF session with the O-RU, e.g., triggered by some out of band 14 interaction with the IP address management functionality. In order to support NETCONF clients corresponding to known 15 O-RU Controllers that either do not attempt to initiate a NETCONF session with the O-RU, or are prohibited from doing 16 so, e.g., because of Network Address Translation limitations, the O-RU shall call home to all known O-RU Controllers 17 with which it does not already have an active NETCONF session. 18 If the O-RU is unable to establish a NETCONF session with any of the O-RU Controller(s), the O-RU shall use the “re-19 call-home-no-ssh-timer” to repeatedly re-perform the call home procedure to all with which is does not have an 20 established NETCONF session.  21 The O-RU shall use RFC 8071 [15] whereby the O-RU (NETCONF Server) initiates a TCP connection to the NETCONF 22 client. The port used by the O-RU shall be the one signaled using the draft-ietf-netconf-zerotouch DHCP option, else, if 23 no port was signaled, the O-RU shall use the IANA-assigned port 4334. When the NETCONF client accepts a TCP 24 connection on the allocated port, it initiates an SSH session with the NETCONF Server. Using this SSH session, the 25 NETCONF client initiates a NETCONF session. 26

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        24
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00

ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 The O-RU shall ensure that a persistent connection to any NETCONF client with “sudo” privileges is maintained by 1 actively testing the aliveness of the connection using the keep-alive mechanism defined in [15]. The establishment of 2 NETCONF client privileges is covered in Section 3.4. 3
3.3 SSH Connection Establishment 4 The identity of the SSH server (O-RU) shall be verified and authenticated by the SSH client (NETCONF client) according 5 to local policy before password-based authentication data or any configuration or state data is sent to or received from the 6 SSH server.  7 Public key-based host authentication shall be used for authenticating the server (RFC 4253) by the clients. In addition, 8 server authentication based on X.509 certificates may also be provided [31]. 9 Note: Public key-based host authentication requires that the SSH server (O-RU) public keys are provisioned in the 10 NETCONF client (e.g., O-DU). As an alternative, RFC4251 mentions that “a possible strategy is to only accept a host 11 key without checking the first time a host is connected, save the key in a local database, and compare against that key on 12 all future connections to that host.”. This option simplifies the key management procedure as it doesn’t require to pre-13 populate them in O-DU / NMS (SSH client) but obviously at the price of degraded security, therefore the support of this 14 option shall be configurable and left to operator’s choice. 15 3.3.1 NETCONF Security 16 In this version of O-RU Management Plane Specification, the security of the NETCONF protocol is realized using SSHv2. 17 O-RAN NETCONF implementations shall implement RFC 6242 [5] that addresses earlier delimiter issues when using 18 RFC 4742. 19 As the default, the NETCONF Server in the O-RU shall provide access to the NETCONF subsystem only when using 20 SSH established using the IANA-assigned TCP port 830 [5]. The O-RU may be configured to additionally allow access 21 to the NETCONF subsystem over other ports. 22 If multiple NETCONF sessions are established to an O-RU, those sessions shall be established over separate SSH tunnels. 23 3.3.2 NETCONF Authentication 24 This version of the O-RU Management Plane Specification uses password authentication method for SSHv2 [6]. In 25 addition, client authentication based on X.509 certificates may also be provided [31]. 26 Upon initial system initialization, the O-RU is configured with a default account. The specific details of the default 27 account are to be agreed between operator and vendor. An example of a default user account is one with username 28 “oranuser”.  29 The default account may be of account-type PASSWORD, in which case a default password also needs to be defined and 30 configured in the O-RU, for example “o-ran-password”. 31 Note: As the default account may be operator specific, this may require that the O-RU provides facilities to configure 32 securely this default account at installation time (i.e. before the O-RU is connected to the O-DU). 33 The default account is member of the “sudo” access control group (see Section 3.4 for details of groups/privileges) as it 34 can be used to create other accounts (see Section 3.3.3). 35 The identity of the SSH client (NETCONF client) shall be verified and authenticated by the SSH server (O-RU) according 36 to local policy to ensure that the incoming SSH client request is legitimate before any configuration or state data is sent 37 to or received from the SSH client.  38 Upon initial system initialization, the NETCONF client can authenticate itself to the O-RU using SSH Authentication, 39 with the agreed default username and password. 40 If authentication based on X.509 certificates according to [31] is supported by SSH client and server, the certificates need 41 to be installed at initial system initialization, or can be obtained through certificate enrolment with operator’s PKI 42 (certificate enrolment as defined by 3GPP with CMPv2 protocol between the NE and the operator’s CA). 43

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        26
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 For the purposes of user authentication, the mapping between certificates and user names is provided by the 1 SubjectAltName field of the X.509 certificate, which means that the user name is coded in the subjectAltName. The 2 username is determined from the subjectAltName using the rules defined in RFC 7589. 3 3.3.3 User Account Provisioning 4 The NETCONF client with suitable privileges may provision user accounts on the O-RU, including the accounts (users) 5 name, password, group (see Section 3.4 for details of groups/privileges) and whether a particular account is enabled or 6 disabled.  7 • The username name is a string between 3-32 characters. The first character must be a lowercase letter. The remaining 8 characters can be a lowercase letter or a number.  9 • The account-type is an enumeration, indicating whether password or certificate-based authentication is used for this 10 account. 11 • The password is a string between 8-128 characters. Allowed characters in the password field include lowercase and 12 uppercase letters, numbers and the special characters: ! $ % ^ ( ) _ + ~ { } [ ] . – The password leaf is not present for 13 those user accounts associated with certificate-based authentication. 14 • The access control group associated with an account (see Section 3.4 for details of groups/privileges). 15 • Whether an account is enabled. The YANG model ensures that at least one user account is always enabled on the O-16 RU 17 The new account information (user name, password, access group and whether the account is enabled) shall be stored 18 in reset-persistent memory in O-RU. 19 When certificate based client authentication is used no password needs to be provisioned. At time of SSH connection, 20 user’s authorization is done based on the X.509 certificate’s SubjectAltName field that codes the associated account’s 21 name. 22 When other user account (sudo) is created, the NETCONF client closes existing NETCONF session as described in 23 section 3.7. Then, the O-RU disables the default account and default account stays disabled over the resets. The default 24 account becomes enabled when the O-RU is reset to the factory default software by following the procedures defined in 25 subsection 5.7. Any other way to enable the default account is not precluded as O-RU vendor implementation matter. 26
3.4 NETCONF Access Control 27 This subsection provides the access control for NETCONF client. Its motivation is when multiple NETCONF clients 28 (users) exist, access control mechanism allows to limit some operation for one client but full access for the other client. 29 In particular, for hybrid access configuration as introduced in Chapter 2, this allows the privileges associated with the 30 NETCONF client in the O-DU to be distinct and different from the privileges associated with the NETCONF client in the 31 NMS 32 In order to support interoperable access control management, the NETCONF Server shall use the IETF NETCONF Access 33 Control Model [RFC8341]. 34 Currently four access control groups are defined per SSH session: “sudo”, “nms”, “fm-pm”, and “swm”. The table below 35 maps the group name to different privileges. Privileges are defined per namespace for read “R”, write “W” and execute 36 “X” rpc operations or subscribe to Notifications.  37 Note: when operating in hybrid management, the definition of above groupings does not preclude the NETCONF client 38 in a centralized network management system from being configured with “sudo” privileges. However, importantly the 39 operation of the O-DU in those situations may not be defined. For example, an O-DU when operating with an O-RU 40 which receives an autonomous reset RPC from a centralized NMS may not result in the O-DU recovering the o-ran-41 operations:operational-info/operational-state/restart-cause from the O-RU to then determine that an NMS triggered 42 reset has been performed. 43

3.5 NETCONF capability discovery 4 The O-RU advertises its NETCONF capabilities in the NETCONF Hello message. The Hello message provides an 5 indication of support for standard features defined in NETCONF RFCs as well as support for specific namespaces. 6 NETCONF capabilities are exchanged between the O-RU and the NETCONF client(s). Examples of capabilities include 7 [3]: 8 • Writable-running Capability 9 • Candidate Configuration Capability and associated Commit operation 10 • Discard change operation 11 • Lock and un-lock operations 12 • Confirmed commit Capability 13 • Cancel commit operation 14 • Rollback on error capability 15 • Validate Capability 16 • Startup configuration capability 17 • URL capability 18 • XPATH capability 19 • Notifications 20 • Interleave capability 21 All O-RAN O-RUs shall support NETCONF Notifications and at least one of the writeable-running and candidate 22 configuration capabilities. 23 The NETCONF client uses the get RPC together with sub-tree based <filter> and XPATH based <filter> to recover 24 particular sub-trees from the O-RU. Please see Chapter 6 for more information on NETCONF based configuration 25 management. 26 In order to avoid interactions between the operation of supervision watchdog timer (see section 3.6) and the confirmed 27 commit timer (default value set to 600 seconds in RFC 6241), when using the NETCONF confirmed commit capability, 28 a NECTONF client with “sudo” privileges shall ensure the confirmed-timeout is less than the supervision-notification-29 interval timer (default value 60 seconds in o-ran-supervision.yang).  30
3.6 Monitoring NETCONF connectivity 31 When having a session with a NETCONF client with “sudo” access privileges, the O-RU operates a watchdog supervision 32 timer to ensure that the session to the NECTONF client is persistent, as described in section 3.4. Additionally, the O-RU 33 provides NETCONF Notifications to indicate to remote systems that its management system is operational. This 34 supervision is intended to be used with the NETCONF client associated with the operation of the peer to the O-RAN 35 Radio’s lower layer split. 36 Note, if another NETCONF client is discovered or configured, e.g., in hybrid mode of operation, and is used to 37 establish a NETCONF session with “sudo” access privileges, the same watchdog supervision rules shall apply, 38 meaning that a NETCONF server shall support the operation of individual supervision watchdog timers for each 39 NETCONF client with “sudo” privileges . 40

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        29
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 The NETCONF client is responsible for automatically enabling the operation of the supervision watchdog timer by 1 creating supervision-notification subscription, whenever the O-RU has an established NETCONF session to a client with 2 “sudo” privileges. After it has been enabled, the O-RU is responsible for repeatedly sending supervision-notification. 3 After that NETCONF client is responsible for sending supervision-watchdog-reset RPC and server shall send next 4 notification timestamp in reply. When resetting the watchdog timer, the NETCONF client may configure a new value for 5 the watchdog timer. Client can set new value of watchdog timer without receiving supervision-notification from O-RU. 6 New value is taken into use immediately with respect to supervision-watchdog-reset RPC content. Next notification 7 should be expected not later than at the moment addressed in timestamp provided by RPC reply. 8 If the watchdog timer expires, the O-RU will enter “supervision failure” condition, as described in Chapter 11. If all 9 NETCONF sessions to NECTONF clients with “sudo” privileges are closed, the O-RU shall immediately disable 10 operation of the supervision timer. 11

3.7 Closing a NETCONF Session 4 A NETCONF client closes an existing NETCONF session by issuing the RPC close-session command. The O-RU shall 5 respond and close the SSH session. The O-RU shall then re-commence call home procedures, as described in Section 3.2. 6 Note, under normal operations, it is expected that at least one NETCONF session with “sudo” privileges is long-lived and 7 used to repeatedly reset the O-RU’s supervision watchdog timer. 8 Note, if a NETCONF client has been previously become known to an O-RU by being configured using NETCONF, and 9 the NETCONF client is subsequently removed from the O-RU’s configuration, e.g., by a second NETCONF client with 10 “sudo” privileges, the NETCONF server shall force the termination of the NETCONF session to the removed client.  11
Chapter 4 O-RU to O-DU Interface Management  12 An O-RU has a number of network interfaces, including Ethernet, VLAN and IP interfaces. This section describes the 13 management of these network interfaces.  14
4.1 O-RU Interfaces 15 The O-RU’s configuration for its interfaces is defined using the o-ran-interfaces.yang module. This module augments the 16 standard ietf-interfaces.yang and ietf-ip.yang modules. The O-RU’s interfaces are built on a layering principle where each 17 interface has a unique name.  18 All interfaces are referenced by their port-number and name. The base interface corresponds to the Ethernet interface. 19 These leafs describe the maximum transmission unit (l2-mtu), the hardware-address as well as optional alias mac 20 addresseses that may be used to transport the CU plane. Above the Ethernet interface are VLAN interfaces. Both Ethernet 21 and VLAN interfaces can support IP interfaces. IP interfaces are defined using the standard ietf-ip.yang model. 22 Accordingly, each IP interface can have an IPv4 and/or optional IPv6 interface(s) defined. Operational state associated 23 with these interfaces provide additional detail of the layer 3 configuration, including prefix(es), domain name servers and 24 default gateway addresses. 25 Finally, leafs associated with CoS and DSCP marking are defined, enabling independent configuration of CoS and DSCP 26 markings for u-plane, c-plane and m-plane traffic. As a default, all user-plane flows are marked identically by the O-RU. 27 Optionally, the interfaces can be configured to support enhanced user plane marking for up-link traffic whereby different 28 CoS or DSCP values can be configured. This enables individual receive endpoints in the O-RU to be configured with 29 different markings to then enable differentiated handling of up-link flows by the transport system. 30 Because the o-ran-interfaces model defines augments to the ietf-interfaces model, the O-RU can leverage the definition 31 of operational state in ietf-interfaces to optionally report packet and byte counts on a per interface basis. A single RPC is 32 defined in the o-ran-interfaces module, to enable these counters to be reset. 33
4.2 Transceiver 34 The o-ran-transceiver YANG module is used to define operational state for the pluggable transceiver module (like SFP, 35 SFP+, SFP28, XFP and QSFP, QSFP+, QSFP28, QSFP56). Each transceiver is associated with a unique interface-name 36 and port-number. 37 A digital diagnostic monitoring interface for optical transceivers is used to allow access to device operating parameters. 38 As specified in SFF-8472 [16] and SFF-8636 [34], data is typically retrieved from the transceiver module in a file. This 39 file may be obtained from O-RU by the NETCONF client. Please see Chapter 9 for more details. 40 With QSFP form factor, the optical links may be multi-wavelengths (4xTx & 4xRx) and/or multi-fibers (MPO - Multifiber 41 Parallel Optic). The QSFP digital diagnostic interface [34] describes the use of optical lanes and the O-RU interface 42 management defines alarm 29 :“transceiver fault” for all media lanes. 43

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        32
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 The byte with offset i (i=0, …, 511) from the beginning of the file is the byte read from data address i of the transceiver 1 memory at two-wire interface address 0xA0 if i<256, otherwise it is the byte read from data address i-256 of the 2 transceiver memory at two-wire interface address 0xA2. The retrieved data is stored in the file without any conversion in 3 binary format. 4 The O-RU stores data from the transceiver module on transceiver module detection during startup. The data from the 5 transceiver module is saved in the file. A NETCONF client can upload it by using the File Upload procedure defined in 6 chapter 9. The O-RU does not synchronize contents of the file with transceiver memory in runtime, therefore bytes 7 representing dynamic information are expected to be outdated. The O-RU does not remove the file on transceiver module 8 removal. If a transceiver module is inserted during File Upload procedure then the procedure may provide a file with 9 previous content or fail (with failure reason as listed in File Upload procedure). If the O-RU is unable to retrieve the data 10 from the transceiver module or it is not present then the O-RU does not create the file or removes the file created earlier 11 (note that File Upload procedure requesting non-existing file shall fail).  12 The file name shall have the following syntax: 13 sfp_{portNumber}.sffcap 14 where {portNumber} is the value of port-number leaf of the corresponding list of port-transceiver data. Examples: 15 sfp_0.sffcap, sfp_1.sffcap. 16
4.3 C/U Plane VLAN Configuration 17 Within the o-ran-interfaces YANG model, each named Ethernet interface includes a leaf to indicate whether VLAN 18 tagging is supported. By default, VLAN tagging is enabled on all interfaces. This permits an O-RU to autonomously 19 discover that it is connected to a trunk port, as described in Section 3.1.2. 20 When an O-RU is connected to a trunk port, VLANs will also typically be assigned to the C/U plane connections. The 21 VLAN(s) used to support C/U plane transport may be different from the VLAN(s) used to support management plane 22 connectivity. When different VLANs are used, the C/U plane VLANs shall be configured in the O-RU by the NETCONF 23 client. In such circumstances, as defined in o-ran-interfaces, the NETCONF client shall configure separate named 24 interfaces for each active VLAN. This configuration will define a C/U-Plane named VLAN interface as being the higher-25 layer-if reference for the underlying Ethernet interface and the underlying Ethernet interface is defined as being the 26 lower-layer-if reference for the named VLAN interface. 27
4.4 O-RU C/U Plane IP Address Assignment 28 In this release, the support for C/U plane transport over UDP/IP is optional and hence this section only applies to those 29 O-RUs that support this optional capability. 30 An O-RU that supports C/U plane transport over UDP/IP shall support IPv4 and optionally IPv6 based transport. A 31 NETCONF client can determine whether an O-RU supports IPv6 by using the get rpc to recover the list of interfaces 32 supported by the O-RU and using the presence of the augmented ipv6 container in the o-ran-interfaces YANG module to 33 indicate IPv6 is supported. 34 The IP interface(s) used to support UDP/IP based C/U plane transport may be different than the IP interface(s) used to 35 support management plane connectivity. When different IP interface(s) is/are used, the C/U plane IP interfaces shall be 36 configured in the O-RU by the NETCONF client by using the ietf-ip YANG model to configure the IPv4 container and/or 37 IPv6 container. When defined by the NETCONF client, this interface shall be configured using either a named Ethernet 38 interface (i.e., where the interface type is set to ianaift:ethernetCsmacd) and/or a named VLAN interface (i.e., where 39 the interface type is set to ianaift:l2vlan), depending upon whether VLANs are used to support IP based C/U plane 40 traffic. 41 When a separate C/U plane IP interface is configured by the NETCONF client, additionally the NETCONF client may 42 statically configure the IP address(es) on this/these interface(s). If the NETCONF client does not statically configure an 43 IP address, the O-RU shall be responsible for performing IP address assignment procedures on the configured interfaces. 44 When an O-RU has not been configured with a static IP address, the O-RU shall support the IP address assignment using 45 the following techniques: 46 1. IPv4 configuration using DHCPv4 [10]. 47

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        33
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 And when the O-RU supports IPv6: 1 2. IPv6 Stateless Address Auto-Configuration (SLAAC) [11]. 2 3. IPv6 State-full address configuration uses DHCPv6 [12]. 3
4.5 Definition of processing elements 4 The CU-plane application needs to be uniquely associated with specific data flows. This association is achieved by 5 defining an O-RU “processing element” which can then be associated with a particular C/U plane endpoint address [2] or 6 delay measurement operation. 7 The O-RU management plane supports different options for defining the transport-based endpoint identifiers used by a 8 particular processing element (used depending on transport environment), supporting the following 3 options: 9 • Processing element definition based on usage of different (alias) MAC addresses; 10 • Processing element definition based on a combination of VLAN identity and MAC address; and 11 • Processing element definition based on UDP-ports and IP addresses. 12 Note: there is no well-defined source port currently allocated by IANA for the o-ran application and hence the 13 NETCONF client is responsible for configuring this port number in the O-RU.  14 A processing element defines both the local and remote endpoints used with a specific data flow. The processing element 15 definition includes its element name which is then used by other systems to refer to a particular processing element 16 instance. 17 The o-ran-interfaces YANG model is used to define feature support for C/U plane transport based on alias MAC addresses 18 and UDP/IP. The exchange of NETCONF capabilities is used to signal which optional capabilities are supported by the 19 O-RU, as described in Annex C. 20 The o-ran-processing-elements YANG model uses a processing-elements container to define a list of processing 21 elements. Each processing element is identified by a unique element name. Each processing element references a 22 particular interface-name used to support the data flows associated with a particular processing element. Depending 23 upon the type of C/U plane transport session, additionally leafs are configured that specify MAC addresses, and/or VLANs 24 and/or IP addresses and/or UDP ports used to identify a particular processing element. 25
4.6 O-DU Verification of C/U Plane Transport Connectivity  26 As described above, there will likely be multiple C/U-plane data flows being exchanged between the O-DU and the O-27 RU. In order to enable checks verifying end-to-end transport connectivity between the O-DU and O-RU, the O-RU shall 28 support transport connectivity check capabilities using a request/reply function.  29 Using that connectivity monitoring procedure, reachability/connectivity checks between user plane endpoints can be 30 performed by the O-DU: 31 - During O-RU configuration, to validate the transport configuration 32 - At runtime to monitor network connectivity 33 In packet networks connectivity checking is usually done by exchanging probe-messages between the endpoints. The 34 periodicity of this exchange depends on the use case. For availability measurement, the only use case relevant for this 35 specification, the periodicity is usually between 1 and 60 seconds. 36 Two different network protocols are defined for performing the transport connectivity check procedure: 37 - For C/U sessions over Ethernet: Loop-back Protocol (LB/LBM) as defined by IEEE 802.1Q (amendment 38 802.1ag) [17]. 39 - When the O-RU supports C/U sessions over IP: UDP echo, RFC 862 [18]. 40

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        34
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00
 1 Figure 8: C/U Plane Transport Connectivity Verification 2 4.6.1 Ethernet connectivity monitoring procedure 3 If the O-RU and O-DU are operating their C/U sessions on Ethernet, the transport connectivity verification checks operate 4 at the Ethernet layer. In this O-RU Management Plane Specification, the protocol for Ethernet connectivity monitoring is 5 based on the Loop-back Protocol as defined by IEEE 802.1Q (amendment 802.1ag) [17].  6 For the purpose of connectivity monitoring all C/U -plane messaging endpoints in the fronthaul network are part of the 7 same Maintenance Entity (ME). They each get the assigned the role of a Maintenance association End Point (MEP) for 8 LBM. 9 The sending of Loop-back Messages (LBMs) is administratively initiated and stopped in the O-DU. Therefore, sending 10 LBM requests needs to be requested by an administration entity, specifying an Ethernet MAC address assigned to the O-11 RU responder. In this O-RU Management Plane Specification requests are always sent from an O-DU to a unicast Ethernet 12 MAC address of an O-RU. 13 4.6.1.1 Validating the transport configuration 14 After setting up a U/C-plane session between an O-DU and an O-RU, the O-DU can test whether connectivity exists as 15 per the configuration. To achieve that, at the time a U/C-plane messaging endpoint becomes operational at an O-RU, it 16 starts an LBM responder application which automatically responds to incoming LBM requests on that endpoint. Based 17 on a configuration command the O-DU starts sending out a predefined number of LBM requests to its O-RU(s) at a 18 predefined interval, storing the information received in LBM responses from the O-RU(s) in an internal database. O-19 RU(s) are identified by both Ethernet MAC address and the CU plane VLAN.  20 Note, the O-RU shall be able to respond to LoopBack Messages received from different remote Maintenance Association 21 Endpoints 22 In case the configuration of the session is indeed correct, the O-DU should receive LBM responses from the O-RU(s) 23 within a time frame dependent on the network latency and the O-RU’s reaction time. If LBMs from the O-RU(s) are being 24 received, the session is determined to be operational. 25

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        35
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00
4.6.1.2 Monitor network connectivity 1 After the procedure described in section 4.6.1.1 has been executed successfully, a further procedure may be executed 2 continuously to maintain the connectivity status. To achieve this the O-DU continues to send out LBM requests at the 3 configured interval. It also keeps track of LBM responses received.  4 Based on the LBM responses received the O-DU shall decide on the connectivity status. Connectivity shall be assumed 5 to be available as long as LBM responses from the O-RU(s) are being received at the configured interval. Connectivity 6 shall be assumed not available if no LBM response from the particular O-RU has been received for an interval that is as 7 long as 3 x the configured LBM request interval or longer. 8 4.6.1.3 Managing Ethernet connectivity monitoring procedure 9 An O-DU may have one or more Ethernet interfaces that have to support the Ethernet connectivity monitoring procedure. 10 This section describes the management of this function. The module described here is based on (i.e. a subset of) the mef-11 cfm module defined by the Metro Ethernet Forum [19]. This is to allow for a later extension of the module to the full 12 feature set of mef-cfm.  13 The YANG module provided below supports the configuration and fault management of the Loop-back Protocol as 14 defined by IEEE 802.1Q (amendment 802.1ag). 15 Derived from MEF CFM YANG, the subset of type definitions are defined as part of the o-ran-lbm.yang. 16 4.6.2 IP connectivity monitoring procedure 17 If the O-RU and O-DU are connected using IP (and UDP/IP is being used to transport the C/U plane), these transport 18 connectivity verification checks operate at layer 3. Layer 3 connection verification is based on the O-RU supporting the 19 UDP echo server functionality, RFC 862 [18]. The NETCONF client is responsible for enabling the UDP echo server in 20 the O-RU, triggering the O-RU to listen for UDP datagrams on the well-known port 7. When a datagram is received by 21 the O-RU, the data from it is sent back towards the sender, where its receipt can be used to confirm UDP/IP connectivity 22 between the endpoints. 23 4.6.2.1 Managing IP Connectivity Monitoring Procedure 24 This section describes the management of the UDP echo functionality. The NETCONF client uses the enable-udp-echo 25 leaf in the udp-echo YANG model to control operation of the UDP echo server in the O-RU. The NETCONF client is 26 able to control the DSCP marking used by the O-RU when it echoes back datagrams using the dscp-config leaf. 27 Additionally, the NETCONF client can recover the number of UDP Echo messages sent by the O-RU by using echo-28 replies-transmitted operational state. 29 An O-DU may have one or more IP interfaces that have to support the UDP/IP connectivity monitoring procedure. An 30 O-RU with its UDP echo server enabled shall be able to respond to UDP datagrams originated from any valid source IP 31 address. 32 4.7 C/U-Plane Delay Management  33 The Intra-PHY lower layer fronthaul split has the characteristic of a stringent bandwidth and tight latency requirement. 34 The CUS-Plane specification [2] describes how the propagation delay incurred due to distance between the O-DU and O-35 RU is an important parameter in defining the optimization of windowing and receive-side buffering operations. This 36 section describes the procedures that are used to manage the delay parameters for the fronthaul split.  37 4.7.1 Delay Parameters 38 The reference points for delay management are introduced in [2] and included for reference in Figure 9. 39

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        36
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00
 1 Figure 9: Definition of reference points for delay management 2 Important delay parameters related to the operation of the O-RU are referred to as the O-RU delay profile. As the delay 3 characteristics for an O-RU may vary based on air interface properties, a table of the parameters is provided based on a 4 combination of sub-carrier spacing (SCS) and channel bandwidth. When considering the downlink data direction, these 5 parameters include: 6 T2a_min :  Corresponding to the minimum O-RU data processing delay between receiving the last data sample over 7 the fronthaul interface and transmitting the first IQ sample at the antenna. 8 T2a_max: Corresponding to the earliest allowable time when a data packet is received before the corresponding firs 9 IQ sample is transmitted at the antenna. 10 Using the above parameters, (T2a_max – T2a_min): The difference between these two parameters corresponds to the 11 O-RU reception window range. 12 T2a_min_cp_dl: Corresponding to the minimum O-RU data processing delay between receiving downlink real time 13 control plane message over the fronthaul interface and transmitting the corresponding first IQ sample at the antenna. 14 T2a_max_cp_dl: Corresponding to the earliest allowable time when a downlink real time control message is received 15 before the corresponding first IQ sample is transmitted at the antenna. 16 Tcp_adv_dl: Corresponding to the time difference (advance) between the reception window for downlink real time 17 Control messages and reception window for the corresponding IQ data messages. 18 The delay parameters related to the operation of the O-RU for the uplink data direction include: 19 Ta3_min: Corresponding to the minimum O-RU data processing delay between receiving an IQ sample at the antenna 20 and transmitting the first data sample over the fronthaul interface. 21 Ta3_max: Corresponding to the maximum O-RU data processing delay between receiving an IQ sample at the antenna 22 and transmitting the last data sample over the fronthaul interface. 23 Using the above parameters, (Ta3_max – Ta3_min): The difference between these two parameters corresponds to the 24 O-RU transmission window range. 25 T2a_min_cp_ul: The minimum O-RU data processing delay between receiving real time up-link control plane 26 message over the fronthaul interface and receiving the first IQ sample at the antenna. 27 T2a_max_cp_ul: The earliest allowable time when a real time up-link control message is received before the 28 corresponding first IQ sample is received at the antenna. 29 When requested, all O-RUs shall signal the table of statically “pre-defined” values of the above parameters, for different 30 supported combinations of SCS and channel bandwidth over the management plane interface. This will typically occur 31 during the initial startup phase. 32 4.7.2 Reception Window Monitoring 33 The O-RU shall monitor operation of its reception window, monitoring the arrival of packets received over the fronthaul 34 interface relative to the earliest and latest allowable times as defined by the values of T2a_max and T2a_min respectively. 35 See Annex B.2 for information on reception window counters. 36 4.8 O-RU Adaptive Delay Capability 37 O-RUs may optionally support the ability to optimize their buffers based on information signalled concerning the 38 configuration of the O-DU, e.g., including the O-DU delay profile, together with transport delay information, which may 39

Copyright © 2019 by the O-RAN Alliance.  Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        37
ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00.00 have been derived by the O-DU by using a delay measurement procedures operated by the O-DU or by other techniques. 1 This section describes such optional O-RU buffer optimization functionality.  2 An O-RU that supports the optional adaptive timing capability shall indicate such to the O-RU Controller client by 3 exchanging NETCONF capabilities, as described in Section 6.2 and Annex C indicating that it supports the ADAPTIVE-4 O-RU-PROFILE feature. An O-RU Controller may then provide the O-RU with the O-DU delay profile based on a 5 combination of sub-carrier spacing (SCS) and channel bandwidth, comprising the following parameters: 6 T1a_max_up: Corresponding to the earliest possible time which the O-DU can support transmitting an IQ data 7 message prior to transmission of the corresponding IQ samples at the antenna 8 TXmax: Corresponding to the maximum amount of time which the O-DU requires to transmit all downlink user plane 9 IQ data message for a symbol. 10 Ta4_max: Corresponding to the latest possible time which the O-DU can support receiving the last uplink user plane 11 IQ data message for a symbol 12 RXmax: Corresponding to the maximum time difference the O-DU can support between receiving the first user plane 13 IQ data message for a symbol and receiving the last user plane IQ data message for the same symbol. 14 In addition to the O-DU delay profile, the O-RU-Controller provides the O-RU with the transport network timing 15 parameters: 16 T12_min: Corresponding to the minimum delay between any O-DU and O-RU processing elements 17 T12_max:  Corresponding to the maximum delay between O-DU and O-RU processing elements 18 T34_min: Corresponding to the minimum delay between any O-RU and O-DU processing elements 19 T34_max:  Corresponding to the maximum delay between O-RU and O-DU processing elements 20 The O-RU may use this information to adapt its delay profile, ensuring that the inequalities defined in Annex B of [2] are 21 still valid  22 The O-RU controller will typically provide this information during the O-RU’s start-up procedure. If an O-RU receives 23 the adaptive delay configuration information when operating a carrier, the O-RU shall not adapt its O-RU delay profile 24 until all carriers operating using the O-RU buffers have been disabled. Once an O-RU has adapted its O-RU profile, it 25 will include the newly adapted timing values when signaling its delay parameters to a NETCONF client. 26
4.9 Measuring transport delay parameters 27 An O-RU may optionally indicate that it supports the eCPRI based measurement of transport delays between O-DU and 28 O-RU, as described in [2].  29 When indicated that it supports the optional eCPRI based delay measurement, an O-RU shall support the processing of 30 delay measurement messages at any time, including whenever the C/U plane is configured. As well as the O-RU including 31 its timing compensation values in the eCPRI delay measurement messages (tcv1 and tcv2 shown in the figure below), the 32 YANG model additionally enables a NETCONF client to recover these parameters from the O-RU over the M-Plane 33 interface. 34
